            <div style="position:relative; width:45px; z-index:1">
             <div style="width:45px">
              <div style="text-align:center; vertical-align:top; font-size:140%; line-height:2em">
               <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
                <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_affricate" title="Voiceless velar affricate">
                 k͡x
                </a>
               </span>
              </div>
              <div style="height:20px">
               <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:45px">

                </a>
               </span>
              </div>

         </a>
         consonants
        </th>

         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">

         </a>

       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align:center;padding:0em 0.5em;background:#e6e6ff;">
         <span class="nowrap">
          <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
           help
          </a>
         </span>
         •
         <span class="nowrap">
          <img alt="Loudspeaker.svg" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help_(Ogg)" title="Wikipedia:Media help (Ogg)">
           help (audio)
          </a>
         </span>
         •
         <span class="nowrap">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Affricate_ligatures.png" title="File:Affricate ligatures.png">
           image
          </a>
         </span>
         •
         <span class="nowrap">
          <a href="/wiki/Template:IPA_chart_affricate_consonants_with_audio" title="Template:IPA chart affricate consonants with audio">
           template
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       An
       <b>
        affricate
       </b>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        consonant
       </a>
       that begins as a
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
        stop
       </a>
       and releases as a
       <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">
        fricative
       </a>
       , generally with the same
       <a href="/wiki/Place_of_articulation" title="Place of articulation">
        place of articulation
       </a>
       (most often
       <a href="/wiki/Coronal_consonant" title="Coronal consonant">
        coronal
       </a>
       ). It is often difficult to decide if a stop and fricative form a single
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phoneme
       </a>
       or a consonant pair.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       English has two affricate phonemes,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t͡ʃ/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d͡ʒ/
       </span>
       , often spelled
       <i>
        ch
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        j
       </i>
       , respectively.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">
        Examples
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Examples">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       sounds spelled "ch" and "j" (
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription#Narrow_versus_broad_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">
        broadly transcribed
       </a>
       as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ʃ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d͡ʒ]
       </span>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
        IPA
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       <i>
        z
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡s]
       </span>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       <i>
        z
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d͡z]
       </span>
       are typical affricates, and sounds like these are fairly common in the world's languages, as are other affricates with similar sounds, such as those in
       <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">
        Polish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       . However, voiced affricates other than
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d͡ʒ]
       </span>
       are relatively uncommon. For several places of articulation they are not attested at all.
      </p>
      <p>
       Much less common are
       <a href="/wiki/Labiodental_consonant" title="Labiodental consonant">
        labiodental
       </a>
       affricates, such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [p͡f]
       </span>
       in German and
       <a href="/wiki/Izi_language" title="Izi language">
        Izi
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">
        velar
       </a>
       affricates, such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k͡x]
       </span>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Tswana_language" title="Tswana language">
        Tswana
       </a>
       (written
       <i>
        kg
       </i>
       ) or in High Alemannic
       <a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">
        Swiss German
       </a>
       dialects. Worldwide, relatively few languages have affricates in these positions even though the corresponding
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
        stop consonants
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [p]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k]
       </span>
       , are common or virtually universal. Also less common are alveolar affricates where the fricative release is
       <a href="/wiki/Lateral_consonant" title="Lateral consonant">
        lateral
       </a>
       , such as the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ɬ]
       </span>
       sound found in
       <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">
        Nahuatl
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Navajo_language" title="Navajo language">
        Navajo
       </a>
       . Some other
       <a href="/wiki/Athabaskan_languages" title="Athabaskan languages">
        Athabaskan languages
       </a>
       , such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dene_Suline_language" title="Dene Suline language">
        Dene Suline
       </a>
       , have unaspirated, aspirated, and ejective series of affricates whose release may be dental, alveolar, postalveolar, or lateral:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̪͡θ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̪͡θʰ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̪͡θʼ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡s]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡sʰ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡sʼ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ʃ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ʃʰ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ʃʼ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ɬ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ɬʰ]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ɬʼ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notation">
        Notation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Notation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Affricates are transcribed in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
        International Phonetic Alphabet
       </a>
       by a combination of two letters, one for the stop element and the other for the fricative element. In order to show that these are parts of a single consonant, a
       <a href="/wiki/Tie_(typography)" title="Tie (typography)">
        tie bar
       </a>
       is generally used. The tie bar appears most commonly above the two letters, but may be placed under them if it fits better there, or simply because this is more legible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         p͡f, t͡s, d͡z, t͡ɬ, d͡ɮ, t͡ʃ, d͡ʒ, t͡ɕ, d͡ʑ, t͡ʂ, d͡ʐ , k͡x
        </span>
        ⟩
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       or
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         p͜f, t͜s, d͜z, t͜ɬ, d͜ɮ, t͜ʃ, d͜ʒ, t͜ɕ, d͜ʑ, t͜ʂ, d͜ʐ , k͜x
        </span>
        ⟩.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       A less common notation indicates the release of the affricate with a superscript:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        .⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         pᶠ, tˢ, dᶻ, tᶴ, dᶾ, kˣ
        </span>
        ⟩
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       This is derived from the IPA convention of indicating other releases with a superscript. However, this convention is more typically used for a fricated release that is too brief to be considered a true affricate.
      </p>
      <p>
       Though they are no longer standard IPA, ligatures are available in
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       for the six common affricates
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        ⟨
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ʦ, ʣ, ʧ, ʤ, ʨ, ʥ
        </span>
        ⟩.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Any of these notations can be used to distinguish an affricate from a sequence of a stop plus a fricative, which exists in some languages such as Polish. However, in languages where there is no such distinction, such as English, the tie bars are commonly dropped.
      </p>
      <p>
       In other phonetic transcription systems, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Americanist_phonetic_notation" title="Americanist phonetic notation">
        Americanist
       </a>
       system, affricates may be transcribed with single letters. The affricates
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡s]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d͡z]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ʃ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d͡ʒ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ɬ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d͡ɮ]
       </span>
       are transcribed respectively as ⟨c⟩ or ⟨¢⟩; ⟨j⟩, ⟨ƶ⟩, or (older) ⟨ʒ⟩; ⟨c⟩ or ⟨č⟩; ⟨ǰ⟩, ⟨ǧ⟩, or (older) ⟨ǯ⟩; ⟨ƛ⟩; and ⟨λ⟩ or ⟨dl⟩. Within the IPA,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dʒ]
       </span>
       are sometimes transcribed with the symbols for the palatal stops, ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        c
       </span>
       ⟩ and ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        ɟ
       </span>
       ⟩.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Affricates_vs._stop.E2.80.93fricative_sequences">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Affricates_vs._stop–fricative_sequences">
        Affricates vs. stop–fricative sequences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Affricates vs. stop–fricative sequences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In some languages, affricates contrast phonemically with stop–fricative sequences:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">
         Polish
        </a>
        affricate
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t͡ʂ/
        </span>
        in
        <i>
         czysta
        </i>
        'clean
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender#Masculine,_feminine,_and_neuter" title="Grammatical gender">
         (f.)
        </a>
        ' versus stop–fricative
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʂ/
        </span>
        in
        <i>
         trzysta
        </i>
        'three hundred'.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-3">
          [3]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Klallam_language" title="Klallam language">
         Klallam
        </a>
        affricate
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t͡s/
        </span>
        in
        <i>
         k’ʷə́nc
        </i>
        'look at me' versus stop–fricative
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ts/
        </span>
        in
        <i>
         k’ʷə́nts
        </i>
        'he looks at it'.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The exact phonetic difference varies between languages. In stop–fricative sequences, the stop has a release burst before the fricative starts; but in affricates, the fricative element
       <i>
        is
       </i>
       the release. Phonologically, stop–fricative sequences may have a
       <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">
        syllable
       </a>
       boundary between the two segments, but not necessarily.
      </p>
      <p>
       In English,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ts/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /dz/
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        nuts
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        nods
       </i>
       ) are considered phonemically stop–fricative sequences because they usually contain a
       <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">
        morpheme
       </a>
       boundary (for example,
       <i>
        nuts
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        nut
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       ), but the sounds are phonetically affricates.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The English affricate phonemes
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t͡ʃ/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d͡ʒ/
       </span>
       are generally not at a morpheme boundary. Depending on dialect, English speakers may distinguish an affricate from a stop–fricative sequence in some contexts such as when the sequence occurs across syllable boundaries:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         cat shit
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kæt.ʃɪt/
        </span>
        →
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kʰæʔʃɪt̚]
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         catch it
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kæt͡ʃ.ɪt/
        </span>
        →
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [kʰæt͡ʃɪt̚]
        </span>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       there
       <a href="/wiki/Debuccalization" title="Debuccalization">
        debuccalizes
       </a>
       to a
       <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">
        glottal stop
       </a>
       before
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʃ/
       </span>
       in many dialects, making it phonetically distinct from
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t͡ʃ/
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       One
       <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_phonetics" title="Acoustic phonetics">
        acoustic
       </a>
       criterion for differentiating affricates and stop–fricative sequences is the rate of
       <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">
        amplitude
       </a>
       increase of the frication noise, which is known as the
       <i>
        rise time
       </i>
       . Affricates have a short rise time to the peak frication amplitude; stop–fricative sequences have longer rise times (Howell &amp; Rosen 1983, Johnson 2003, Mitani et al. 2006).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_affricates">
        List of affricates
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: List of affricates">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In the case of coronals, the symbols ⟨
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        t, d
       </span>
       ⟩ are normally used for the stop portion of the affricate regardless of place. For example,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t͡ʂ]
       </span>
       is commonly seen for
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʈ͡ʂ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The exemplar languages are ones that have been reported to have these sounds, but in several cases they may need confirmation.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sibilant_affricates">

         </a>

         </a>

          <div style="width:180px">
           <div style="text-align:center; vertical-align:top; font-size:120%; line-height:1.5em">

             </a>
            </span>

            <div class="mediaContainer" style="width:180px">
             <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-disablecontrols="timedText" data-durationhint="1.6486167800454" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Voiced_retroflex_affricate.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_25" preload="none" style="width:180px">

         Mandarin
         <i>

         </i>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
          pinyin

         </i>

         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Caucasian_languages" title="Northwest Caucasian languages">
        Northwest Caucasian
       </a>
       languages
       <a href="/wiki/Abkhaz_phonology" title="Abkhaz phonology">
        Abkhaz
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ubykh_phonology" title="Ubykh phonology">
        Ubykh
       </a>
       both contrast sibilant affricates at four places of articulation: alveolar, postalveolar, alveolo-palatal and retroflex. They also distinguish voiceless, voiced, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ejective" title="Ejective">
        ejective
       </a>
       affricates at each of these.
      </p>
      <p>
       When a language only has one type of affricate, it is usually a sibilant; this is the case in e.g. some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       dialects (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d̠ʒ]
       </span>
       ), most dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̠ʃ]
       </span>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">
        Thai
       </a>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̠ɕ]
       </span>
       ).
      </p>

         </a>

         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nahuatl_language" title="Nahuatl language">
          Nahuatl
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Navajo_language" title="Navajo language">
          Navajo
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Tswana_language" title="Tswana language">
          Tswana
         </a>
         , etc.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_lateral_affricate" title="Voiced alveolar lateral affricate">

         </a>

         </a>

         </a>

         </span>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Trilled_affricates">

       <span class="mw-editsection">

        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Heterorganic_affricates">
        Heterorganic affricates
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Heterorganic affricates">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Although most affricates are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homorganic" title="Homorganic">
        homorganic
       </a>
       , Navajo and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiricahua_language" title="Chiricahua language">
        Chiricahua Apache
       </a>
       have a heterorganic alveolar-velar affricate
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tx]
       </span>
       (McDonough &amp; Ladefoged 1993, Hoijer &amp; Opler 1938).
       <a href="/wiki/Wari%E2%80%99_language" title="Wari’ language">
        Wari’
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pirah%C3%A3_language" title="Pirahã language">
        Pirahã
       </a>
       have a voiceless dental bilabially trilled affricate [t̪ʙ̥] (see
       <a href="#Trilled_affricates">
        #Trilled affricates
       </a>
       ). Other heterorganic affricates are reported for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Sotho" title="Northern Sotho">
        Northern Sotho
       </a>
       (Johnson 2003) and other
       <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">
        Bantu languages
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/Phuthi_language" title="Phuthi language">
        Phuthi
       </a>
       , which has alveolar–labiodental affricates
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tf]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dv]
       </span>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesotho_language" title="Sesotho language">
        Sesotho
       </a>
       , which has bilabial–palatoalveolar afficates
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pʃ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bʒ]
       </span>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Djeoromitxi_language" title="Djeoromitxi language">
        Djeoromitxi
       </a>
       (Pies 1992) has
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ps]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bz]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Phonation.2C_coarticulation_and_other_variants">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonation,_coarticulation_and_other_variants">
        Phonation, coarticulation and other variants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonation, coarticulation and other variants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The more common of the voiceless affricates are all attested as
       <a href="/wiki/Ejective_consonant" title="Ejective consonant">
        ejectives
       </a>
       as well:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tθʼ, tsʼ, tɬʼ, tʃʼ, tɕʼ, tʂʼ, cʎ̥ʼ, kxʼ, kʟ̝̊ʼ]
       </span>
       . Several Khoisan languages such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/!X%C3%B3%C3%B5_language" title="!Xóõ language">
        !Xóõ
       </a>
       are reported to have voiced ejective affricates, but these may actually be consonant clusters:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dtsʼ, dtʃʼ]
       </span>
       . Affricates are also commonly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspiration_(phonetics)" title="Aspiration (phonetics)">
        aspirated
       </a>
       :
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɱp̪fʰ, tθʰ, tsʰ, tɬʰ, tʃʰ, tɕʰ, tʂʰ]
       </span>
       , occasionally
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" title="Breathy voice">
        murmured
       </a>
       :
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɱb̪vʱ, d̠ʒʱ]
       </span>
       , and sometimes
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prenasalized_stop" title="Prenasalized stop">
        prenasalized
       </a>
       :
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ⁿdz, ⁿdzʱ, ᶯɖʐ, ᶯɖʐʱ]
       </span>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Labialization" title="Labialization">
        Labialized
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">
        palatalized
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">
        velarized
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">
        pharyngealized
       </a>
       affricates also occur. Affricates may also have phonemic length, that is, affected by a
       <a href="/wiki/Chroneme" title="Chroneme">
        chroneme
       </a>
       , as in
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Karelian_language" title="Karelian language">
        Karelian
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonological_representation">
        Phonological representation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phonological representation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg">
          <img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          <b>
           This section needs expansion
          </b>
          .
          <small>
           You can help by
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">
            adding to it
           </a>
           .
          </small>
          <small>
           <i>
            (September 2015)
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In phonology, affricates tend to behave similarly to stops, taking part in phonological patterns that fricatives do not. Kehrein analyzes phonetic affricates as phonological stops.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A sibilant or lateral (and presumably trilled) stop can be realized phonetically only as an affricate and so might be analyzed phonemically as a sibilant or lateral stop. In that analysis, affricates other than sibilants and laterals are a phonetic mechanism for distinguishing stops at similar places of articulation (like more than one labial, coronal, or dorsal place). For example,
       <a href="/wiki/Chipewyan_language" title="Chipewyan language">
        Chipewyan
       </a>
       has laminal dental
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t̪͡θ]
       </span>
       vs. apical alveolar
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [t]
       </span>
       ; other languages may contrast velar
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [k]
       </span>
       with palatal
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [c͡ç]
       </span>
       and uvular
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [q͡χ]
       </span>
       . Affricates may also be a strategy to increase the phonetic contrast between aspirated or ejective and tenuis consonants.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to Kehrein, no language contrasts a non-sibilant, non-lateral affricate with a stop at the same place of articulation and with the same phonation and airstream mechanism, such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t̪/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t̪θ/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /k/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kx/
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Affrication">
        <span id="Affrication">
         Affrication
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Affrication">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Affrication (sometimes called
       <i>
        affricatization
       </i>
       ) is a
       <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">
        sound change
       </a>
       by which a consonant, usually a
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
        stop
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">
        fricative
       </a>
       , changes into an affricate. Examples include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">
         Proto-Germanic
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        &gt; Modern English
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ/
        </span>
        , as in
        <i>
         chin
        </i>
        (cf. German
        <i>
         Kinn
        </i>
        :
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_palatalization" title="Anglo-Frisian palatalization">
         Anglo-Frisian palatalization
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Early Modern English
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tj, dj/
        </span>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ dʒ/
        </span>
        (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yod-coalescence" title="Yod-coalescence">
         yod-coalescence
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /p, t, k/
        </span>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /pf, ts, kx/
        </span>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/High_German_consonant_shift" title="High German consonant shift">
         High German consonant shift
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t]
        </span>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ts, tɕ]
        </span>
        before
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɯᵝ, i]
        </span>
        respectively in 16th-century Japanese
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-6">
          [6]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [r]
        </span>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [dʒ, dʑ]
        </span>
        word-initially in
        <a href="/wiki/Udmurt_language" title="Udmurt language">
         Udmurt
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-7">
          [7]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-affrication">
        <span id="Pre-affrication">
         Pre-affrication
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Affricate_consonant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pre-affrication">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In rare instances, a fricative–stop contour may occur. This is the case in dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       that have velar frication
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˣ]
       </span>
       where other dialects have
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-aspiration" title="Pre-aspiration">
        pre-aspiration
       </a>
       . For example, in the
       <a href="/wiki/Harris,_Scotland" title="Harris, Scotland">
        Harris
       </a>
       dialect there is
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃaˣkʰ]
       </span>
       'seven' and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [əhʷɔˣkʰ]
       </span>
       'eight' (or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃax͜kʰ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [əhʷɔx͜kʰ]
       </span>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
